<div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>Strong gravitational lensing provides a powerful probe of the physical properties of quasars and their host galaxies. A high fraction of the most luminous high-redshift quasars was predicted to be lensed due to magnification bias. However, no multiple imaged quasar was found at <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 in previous surveys. We report the discovery of J043947.08+163415.7, a strongly lensed quasar at <I>z</I>\u00a0=\u00a06.51, the first such object detected at the epoch of reionization, and the brightest quasar yet known at <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05. High-resolution <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> imaging reveals a multiple imaged system with a maximum image separation <I>\u03b8</I>\u00a0~\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2, best explained by a model of three quasar images lensed by a low-luminosity galaxy at <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a00.7, with a magnification factor of ~50. The existence of this source suggests that a significant population of strongly lensed, high-redshift quasars could have been missed by previous surveys, as standard color selection techniques would fail when the quasar color is contaminated by the lensing galaxy.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/870/2/L11&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aaeffe&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaaeffes1\" name=\"apjlaaeffes1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Luminous quasars at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06 allow detailed studies of the evolution of supermassive black holes (SMBHs) and the intergalactic medium (IGM) at early cosmic times. To date, ~150 quasars have been discovered at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a06, with the highest redshift at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a07.54 (Ba\u00f1ados et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib2\">2018</a>). Detections of such objects indicate the existence of billion solar mass (<em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) SMBHs merely a few hundred million years after the first star formation in the universe and provide the most stringent constraints on the theory of early SMBH formation (Volonteri <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib34\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib34\">2012</a>).</p><p>Much of our understanding of the nature of high-redshift quasars assumes that their measured luminosities are intrinsic to the quasars themselves. However gravitational lensing can substantially brighten quasar images. This effect is particularly important in flux-limited surveys, which are sensitive to the brightest sources; the resulting magnification bias (Turner <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib33\">1980</a>) could cause a significant overestimation of the SMBH masses powering these objects. A large lensing fraction among the highest-redshift luminous quasars has long been predicted (Comerford et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib8\">2002</a>; Wyithe &amp; Loeb <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib39\">2002a</a>) and was suggested as a solution to the difficulty in forming billion <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> SMBHs in the early universe. However, the two highest-redshift-known lensed quasars are at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a04.8 (McGreer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib23\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib23\">2010</a>; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib25\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib25\">2016</a>), discovered in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS); no multiple imaged systems were discovered at 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 resolution among the more than 200 quasars at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04\u20136.4 observed in two <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) programs (Richards et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib30\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib30\">2006</a>; McGreer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib22\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib22\">2014</a>). The lack of high-redshift lensed quasars has been a long-standing puzzle. The solution could be either a reduced magnification bias due to a flat quasar luminosity function (Wyithe <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib38\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib38\">2004</a>) or a strong selection effect against lensed objects arising from the morphology or color criteria used in quasar surveys (Wyithe &amp; Loeb <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib40\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib40\">2002b</a>).</p><p>In our wide-area survey of luminous <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a07 quasars (Wang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib35\">2017</a>), we discovered an ultraluminous quasar UHS J043947.08+163415.7 (hereafter J0439+1634) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.51. Subsequent <em>HST</em> imaging shows that it is a multiple imaged gravitationally lensed quasar, the most distant strongly lensed quasar yet known. We present the initial discovery and follow-up imaging observations that confirm its lensing nature in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffes2\">2</a>. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffes3\">3</a>, we present the lensing model in detail. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffes4\">4</a>, we discuss the possibility of a large number of high-redshift lensed quasars missed in previous surveys due to bias in color selection. We use a \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub>M</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.7, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaaeffes2\" name=\"apjlaaeffes2\">2.\u00a0J0439+1634: A Lensed Quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.51</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjlaaeffes2-1\" name=\"apjlaaeffes2-1\">2.1.\u00a0Photometric Selection and Initial Spectroscopy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>J0439+1634 was selected by combining photometric data from the UKIRT Hemisphere Survey (UHS; Dye et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib9\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib9\">2018</a>) in the near-infrared <em>J</em> band, the Pan-STARRS1 survey (PS-1; Chambers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib7\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib7\">2016</a>) at optical wavelengths, and the <em>Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer</em> (<em>WISE</em>; Wright et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib36\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib36\">2010</a>) archive in the mid-infrared. It was chosen as a high-redshift quasar candidate based on it having a <em>z</em>-band dropout signature with <em>z</em><sub><em>AB</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a019.49\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02, <em>y</em><sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0=\u00a017.63\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01, and a red <em>z</em><sub><em>AB</em></sub>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>y</em><sub><em>AB</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.86\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02, along with a blue power-law continuum (<em>J</em><sub>Vega</sub> = 16.52 \u00b1 0.01, <em>y</em><sub>AB</sub> \u2212 <em>J</em><sub>Vega</sub> = 1.11 \u00b1 0.02), and a photometric redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a06.5. The object has a weak <em>i</em>-band detection in PS-1 (<em>i</em><sub>AB</sub> = 21.71 \u00b1 0.05), but is strongly detected in all bands in the Two Micron All Sky Survey (2MASS; Skrutskie et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib32\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib32\">2006</a>), at <em>J</em><sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0=\u00a016.48\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12, <em>H</em><sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0=\u00a015.96\u00a0\u00b1 0.17, and <em>J</em><sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0=\u00a015.06\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.13, respectively, as well in all four <em>WISE</em> bands, with Vega magnitudes of 13.98\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03, 13.24\u00a0\u00b1 0.03, 10.28\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08, and 7.17\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.13, respectively, from W1 to W4 (Schlegel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib42a\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib42a\">1998</a>). This object is in an area of the sky with high galactic extinction with <em>E</em>(<em>B</em>\u2212<em>V</em>)\u00a0=\u00a00.60 (Schlegel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib42a\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib42a\">1998</a>); the <em>J</em><sub>Vega</sub> magnitude in UHS after correcting for extinction becomes 15.98.</p><p>The initial identification spectrum, obtained on 2018 February 6, with the Binospec optical spectrograph (Fabricant et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib11\">2003</a>) on the 6.5 m MMT telescope, shows a prominent break consistent with a strong Ly<em>\u03b1</em> line at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a06.5. Follow-up optical and near-infrared spectra were acquired with MMT/Binospec, the Low Resolution Imaging Spectrograph (LRIS; Oke et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib27\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib27\">1995</a>) on the 10 m Keck I Telescope, and the GNIRS instrument (Elias et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib10\">2006</a>) on the 8.2 m Gemini-North Telescope. The combined optical\u2013IR spectrum is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef1\">1</a>. Strong Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> emission is detected by GNIRS, yielding a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.511\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.003.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaaeffef1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaaeffef1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Combined optical and near-infrared spectrum of the lensed quasar J0439+1634 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.51. The optical portion of the spectrum is from the Binospec instrument on the 6.5 m MMT telescope and the LRIS instrument on the 10 m Keck I telescope. The near-infrared portion of the spectrum is from the GNIRS instrument on the 8.2 m Gemini-North Telescope. The proximity zone around the quasar is denoted by the gray shaded area blueward of Ly<em>\u03b1</em>; its size (<em>R</em><sub><em>p</em></sub> = 3.61 \u00b1 0.15 Mpc) is &gt;2<b>&#x00d7;</b> smaller than for other luminous quasars at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a06.5 (Wu et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib37\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib37\">2015</a>; Mazzucchelli et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib21\">2017</a>), suggesting that the intrinsic ionizing flux is much lower. Insert (a) shows the spectrum in the Ly<em>\u03b1</em> forest region. A faint continuum is clearly detected in the darkest region of the quasar Gunn-Peterson trough, suggesting the presence of a foreground galaxy. Orange dashed lines are the traces of the <em>HST</em>/ACS ramp filters used to image the lensing galaxy and quasar (images shown in Figure <a href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>). Insert (b) shows the Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> region of the quasar spectrum. The red line is the best-fit spectrum when including a power-law continuum, Balmer continuum, and Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>+Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> emission. The best-fit redshift based on Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> is 6.511\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.003. The best-fit FWHM of the Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> line is 2924\u00a0\u00b1\u00a0188 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, yielding an SMBH mass (McLure &amp; Dunlop <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib24\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib24\">2004</a>) of (4.93\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.56)\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>9</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub> before correction for lensing magnification.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaaeffef1\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaaeffef1\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>J0439+1634 is roughly 40% brighter than the luminous <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.30 quasar SDSS J0100+2802 (Wu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib37\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib37\">2015</a>), making it the brightest quasar known at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05. It is also the brightest submillimeter quasar at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05; it is detected by the SCUBA-2 instrument (Holland et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib15\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib15\">2013</a>) on the James Clerk Maxwell Telescope (JCMT) with a total flux of 26.2\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.7 mJy at 850 <em>\u03bc</em>m. However, its high luminosity is likely not intrinsic, but instead boosted via gravitational lensing. The optical spectrum of J0439+1634 shows a faint, continuous trace at <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a09000 \u00c5, visible in the middle of the deepest region of quasar Gunn-Peterson absorption at 8500 \u00c5\u00a0&lt;\u00a09000 \u00c5 (<em>z</em><sub>abs</sub> &gt; 6). This trace extends beyond the quasar Lyman Limit at <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a06840 \u00c5, blueward of the intergalactic medium (IGM) transmission spikes in the quasar Ly<em>\u03b2</em> region. No quasar continuum transmission is expected at these wavelengths due to the extremely high IGM optical depth (Fan et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib13\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib13\">2006</a>), indicating the presence of a foreground object within the 1'' spectroscopic slit. The lensing hypothesis is further supported by the presence of a very small quasar proximity zone (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef1\">1</a>) and an apparent super-Eddington accretion rate based on the Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> measured SMBH mass (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef2\">2</a>), both of which can be explained with a significant lensing magnification.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaaeffef2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaaeffef2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Distribution of quasar bolometric luminosities and SMBH masses estimated from Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> emission. The open red star represents J0439+1634 without lensing correction; the filled red star represents the same object after applying a lensing magnification correction factor of 51<b>&#x00d7;</b> (from the fiducial lensing model in Table <a class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffet1\">1</a>). The green circle represents SDSS J0100+2922 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.30 (Wu et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib37\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib37\">2015</a>), the blue square SDSS J1148+5251 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a06.42 (Fan et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib12\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib12\">2003</a>), and the magenta circles ULAS J1120+0641 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a07.09 (Mortlock et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib26\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib26\">2011</a>) and ULAS J1342+0928 at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a07.54 (Ba\u00f1ados et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib2\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib2\">2018</a>). Black dots denote other <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a06 quasars (Wu et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib37\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib37\">2015</a>; Mazzucchelli et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib21\">2017</a>). The black contours and gray dots show SDSS low-redshift quasars (Shen et al. <a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib31\">2011</a>; with broad absorption line quasars excluded). The error bars represent the 1<em>\u03c3</em> measurement errors. For comparison, the dashed lines illustrate fractions of the Eddington luminosity.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaaeffef2\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaaeffef2\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjlaaeffes2-2\" name=\"apjlaaeffes2-2\">2.2.\u00a0High-resolution Imaging</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>J0439+1634 appears as an unresolved point source on archival PS-1 and UHS images (seeing of ~1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5) and on deeper near-infrared images taken with the Fourstar instrument (Persson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib28\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib28\">2013</a>) on the 6.5 m <em>Magellan</em>-1 Telescope (seeing ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />8). To test the lensing hypothesis, we obtained a high-resolution <em>K</em>-band image using the Advanced Rayleigh guided Ground layer adaptive Optics System (<em>ARGOS</em>; Rabien et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib29\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib29\">2018</a>) on the 2\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a08.4 m Large Binocular Telescope, with a ground-layer adaptive optics (AO) corrected FWHM of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />24. This image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>(A)), taken with the LUCI (Buschkamp et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib5\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib5\">2012</a>) instrument, marginally resolves J0439+1634 beyond the point-spread function (PSF; FWHM\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />30\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />01).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaaeffef3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaaeffef3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0High-resolution images of the strongly lensed quasar J0439+1634 and the best-fit three-image lensing model. A: LBT/<em>ARGOS</em> LUCI image in the <em>K</em> band. With ground-layer AO correction, the FWHM of the PSF is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />24. The quasar image has an FWHM of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />30\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />01. The contours show the image core elongated in the north\u2013south direction as well as excess light toward the east, consistent with the high-resolution <em>HST</em> imaging. B: <em>HST</em>/ACS WFC image with the FR782N ramp filter centered at 7700 \u00c5, covering the quasar Ly<em>\u03b2</em> emission. This \"galaxy+quasar\" image is resolved into at least two pointlike components (A and B) and a faint extended source toward the east (G). C: <em>HST</em>/ACS WFC image with the FR853N ramp filter centered at 8750 \u00c5, covering the deepest part of the quasar Gunn-Peterson trough. This \"galaxy only\" image is used to determine the location and shape parameters of the lensing galaxy. D: best-fit three-image lensing model to the <em>HST</em>/ACS FR782N image, using the lens location and shape derived from the FR853N image. White crosses show the locations of the best-fit quasar images and blue lines show lensing critical curves of the fiducial lensing model. Red lines show the lensing caustics in the source plane. In this model, the total magnification is 51.3\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.4 and the Einstein radius is 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17, which corresponds to a circular velocity of <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn1.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> assuming a lens redshift <span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$z={0.67}_{-0.15}^{+0.19}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z={0.67}_{-0.15}^{+0.19}</script></span></span>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaaeffef3\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaaeffef3\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Even more revealing are the high-resolution observations of J0439+1634 with the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) on the <em>HST</em>, taken on 2018 April 3, using two intermediate-band (\u0394<em>\u03bb</em> ~ 200 \u00c5) ramp filters (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef1\">1</a>). The FR782N observation is centered at 7700 \u00c5, fully covers the quasar Ly<em>\u03b2</em> emission, and is the shortest wavelength at which quasar emission is still detectable, thus providing the highest possible spatial resolution of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />075. The FR853N observation is centered at 8750 \u00c5, within the Gunn-Peterson trough, and images only the foreground galaxy. The \"galaxy+quasar\" FR782N image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>(B)) clearly resolves the system into multiple components: there are at least two point sources separated by 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 and a faint, extended source ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 to the east, which we interpret as the lensing galaxy. The \"galaxy-only\" FR853N image (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>(C)) shows only the lensing galaxy, best fit with an exponential profile, an ellipticity of ~0.65, and an effective radius of ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />4.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjlaaeffes2-3\" name=\"apjlaaeffes2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Properties of the Lensing Galaxy</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We use the best-fit galaxy position and shape parameters from the FR853N image to derive the lensing galaxy flux in the two <em>HST</em> bands and the LBT <em>K</em> band: <em>AB</em><sub>7700\u00c5</sub>\u00a0=\u00a022.40\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.05, <em>AB</em><sub>8750\u00c5</sub>\u00a0=\u00a022.07\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.07, and <em>K</em><sub>Vega</sub>\u00a0=\u00a018.86\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.19. The nondetection in the blue channel of the Keck/LRIS spectrum yields an upper limit of <em>g</em><sub>AB</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a024 for the galaxy. We estimate the synthetic PS-1 <em>g-</em>, <em>r-</em>, and <em>i</em>-band magnitudes of the lensing galaxy using the spectrum of J0439+1634 (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef1\">1</a>), which shows the trace of the lensing galaxy spectrum in the quasar Gunn-Peterson trough. We scale the spectrum by matching it to the <em>HST</em>/FR853N band magnitude, which does not include quasar flux. We choose a wavelength range free of quasar flux, between 8600 and 8900 \u00c5 in the Gunn-Peterson trough, and blueward of the Lyman limit (&lt;6840 \u00c5), to calculate the magnitudes. For the spectrum between 6840 \u00c5 and 8600 \u00c5, we interpolate the continuum by fitting the blue- and red-side spectrum with a spline function. The synthetic <em>g</em>-, <em>r-</em>, and <em>i</em>-band AB magnitudes are estimated to be 25.00\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.90, 23.29\u00a0\u00b10.29, and 22.47\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.11, respectively.</p><p>Based on these photometric data and after applying the Galactic extinction correction (Cardelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib6\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib6\">1989</a>), we estimate the photometric redshift using the EAZY (Brammer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib3\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib3\">2008</a>) code. The peak value of the p(z) probability distribution is z_peak\u00a0=\u00a00.67, and the 1<em>\u03c3</em> confidence interval from the probability distribution is 0.52\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a00.86. With the <em>Le Phare</em> code (Arnouts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib1\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib1\">2002</a>; Ilbert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib16\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib16\">2006</a>) and a set of 12 template galaxies using Bruzual &amp; Charlot (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib4\">2003</a>) models, we find a best-fit stellar mass of 10<sup>9.8</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. Deeper photometry is needed to further improve the photometric redshift and stellar mass determinations.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaaeffes3\" name=\"apjlaaeffes3\">3.\u00a0Lensing Model</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>A purely photometric fit of the <em>HST</em>/ACS FR782N data using only two quasar images has a significant residual, suggesting a more complex lensing configuration. We fit a singular isothermal ellipsoid lensing model, fixing the lens position and ellipticity (<em>e</em> = 0.65) to match the observed galaxy in the FR853N image, while varying the lens mass and position angle along with the source position to reproduce the observed configuration (Keeton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib19\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib19\">2001</a>). We vary the Einstein ring radius and position angle of the galaxy along with the position of the source. For each set of parameters, we solve the lens equation to predict the positions of the images, place copies of the <em>HST</em> PSF at those positions, and compare with the FR782N image to compute a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> goodness of fit. We then use Markov Chain Monte Carlo methods to sample the parameter space. The resulting model is depicted in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a> and the parameters are summarized in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffet1\">1</a>. To interpret the Einstein radius, we assume the galaxy is a thin rotating disk such that the projected ellipticity reflects the inclination, and we compute the corresponding circular velocity (Keeton &amp; Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib20\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib20\">1998</a>). A three-image model is preferred (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>(D)), with a best-fit Einstein radius of <em>\u03b8</em><sub><em>E</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />17\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />01, corresponding to a circular velocity of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn3.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}\\ {\\mathrm{kms}}^{-1}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}\\ {\\mathrm{kms}}^{-1}</script></span></span> and a high total magnification of 51.3\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.4. In this model, the separation of the two brighter images is only 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />04, unresolved even by <em>HST</em>.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjlaaeffet1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nLens Model Parameters\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />168\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />001</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>\u03b8</em><sub><em>E</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />051\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />001</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>\u03b8</em><sub><em>E</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />095\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />001</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn7.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={160}_{-6}^{+8}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn8.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={88}_{-3}^{+4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={88}_{-3}^{+4}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup></td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\n<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn9.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={121}_{-4}^{+6}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={121}_{-4}^{+6}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\u00a0</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.65, PA\u00a0=\u00a0103.1\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.8, PA\u00a0=\u00a0101.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.6</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\"><em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn10.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{PA}}={112.8}_{-7.5}^{+6.0}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{PA}}={112.8}_{-7.5}^{+6.0}</script></span></span>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/suppdata/apjlaaeffet1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aaeffe\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/suppdata/apjlaaeffet1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>We estimate the <em>observed</em> optical luminosity at rest-frame 3000 \u00c5 to be (4.35 \u00b1 0.09)\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>47</sup>\u00a0erg\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup> by fitting the calibrated spectrum. Applying an empirical factor (Shen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib31\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib31\">2011</a>) to convert the luminosity at 3000 \u00c5 to the bolometric luminosity gives <em>L</em><sub>bol</sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.24\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>48</sup> erg\u00a0s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u00a0=\u00a05.85\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>14</sup> <em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. After correction for magnification factor of 51.3, the bolometric luminosity of J0439+1634 is reduced to 1.14\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>13</sup><em>L</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, and the SMBH mass to 4.29\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.60\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>8</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. This corresponds to an Eddington ratio of 0.83\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12.</p><p>However, this model seems to underpredict the flux of the faintest quasar image. It is not clear whether the discrepancy is due to limitations in the current data (e.g., in the <em>HST</em> PSF model) or to fundamental problems with this class of lens models. As an alternative, we consider the possibility that the lens galaxy could actually lie between the quasar images and be blended with them. In this scenario, the galaxy light detected in the <em>HST</em> image could be offset from the mass centroid, due perhaps to strong dust obscuration. For example, if the lensing galaxy is seen mostly edge-on, then we might have detected only the part of the galaxy with the highest surface brightness along the disk. The smallest residuals are obtained for a highly inclined galaxy with projected ellipticity <em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.8, which produces four images and a total magnification of 10.4\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2 (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef4\">4</a> and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffet1\">1</a>). The implied circular velocity <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn11.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={88}_{-3}^{+4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={88}_{-3}^{+4}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> is quite low, comparable to that of the Large Magellanic Cloud. The orientation is consistent with the hypothesis that the observed galaxy light is from part of the disk. It also possible that the nearby galaxy is not related to the lensing. In this case, the true lens galaxy is too faint for detection here, could lie between the quasar images, and be relatively round. We therefore test a third model with ellipticity <em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2, which produces just two images that have a total magnification of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn12.gif\" alt=\"${23.1}_{-0.8}^{+1.4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{23.1}_{-0.8}^{+1.4}</script></span></span>. This model has a modest circular velocity of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffeieqn13.gif\" alt=\"${v}_{c}={121}_{-4}^{+6}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{v}_{c}={121}_{-4}^{+6}</script></span></span> km s<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjlaaeffef4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjlaaeffef4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/2041-8205/870/2/L11/Full/apjlaaeffef4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Fiducial and alternative lens models: fits (top row) and residuals (bottom row) of the <em>HST</em>/ACS FR782N image. As in Figure <a href=\"#apjlaaeffef3\">3</a>, white crosses are locations of quasar images, and blue and red lines represent the lensing critical curves and caustics, respectively.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjlaaeffef4\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjlaaeffef4\" href=\"/2041-8205/870/2/L11/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjlaaeffef4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We consider the fiducial three-image model to be the most likely lensing configuration because it naturally places the center of the lensing galaxy at the position of the detected galaxy image in the two <em>HST</em> bands. However, further observations are needed to clearly distinguish between the different models. Images that are deeper than the current <em>HST</em> observation could fully characterize the lensing galaxy, while observations with higher spatial resolution (possible only with <em>JWST</em> or ALMA) would reveal whether there are two, three, or four image components.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjlaaeffes4\" name=\"apjlaaeffes4\">4.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The probability that a luminous quasar is gravitationally lensed with magnification factor <em>\u03bc</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a02 at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a06 ranges from ~4%, if the bright end of the quasar luminosity function is \u03a6(<em>L</em>)\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>L</em><sup>\u22122.8</sup> (Jiang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib18\">2016</a>), to ~20%, if the quasar luminosity function is as steep as \u03a6(<em>L</em>)\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>L</em><sup>\u22123.6</sup> (Yang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib41\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib41\">2016</a>). Yet J0439+1634 is the first strongly lensed quasar discovered at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 among the several hundred quasars known at this redshift. A reexamination of the color selection used in previous high-redshift quasar surveys suggests a strong selection bias against lensed quasars.</p><p>Selecting <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a06 quasars requires either a nondetection (Jiang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib18\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib18\">2016</a>; Wang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib35\">2017</a>) or a strong drop in the dropout band below the quasar Lyman break (Mazzucchelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib21\">2017</a>). The presence of a lensing galaxy, however, introduces flux into the dropout bands when the image is not fully resolved. Most lensing galaxies are expected to be massive galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a00.5\u20131.5 and to have detectable <em>r</em>- or <em>i</em>-band flux in the SDSS or PS-1 survey. For example, among the 62 lensed <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a04 quasars in the SDSS sample (Inada et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib17\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib17\">2012</a>) with measurements of the lensing galaxy, the faintest lens has <em>i</em><sub><em>AB</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a021.64. On the other hand, the J0439+1634 lens is among the faintest lensing galaxies known, with <em>i</em><sub><em>AB</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a022.47. The faintness of this lens, combined with the high apparent luminosity of the lensed quasar, minimizes the impact of lensing galaxy flux to the overall unresolved quasar+lens color used in candidate selection. If the lens were brighter by even 0.5 mag, J0439+1634 would not have been selected as a high-redshift quasar candidate by our color selection criteria (Wang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib35\">2017</a>; Mazzucchelli et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib21\">2017</a>), suggesting that previous surveys have potentially missed the majority of lensed quasars at the highest redshifts due to their stringent dropout criteria. Thus, a full modeling of quasar+lens colors and selection procedure modifications are needed to cover the majority of the high-redshift lensed quasar population.</p><p>A statistical study of strong lensing properties using the Millennium Simulation (Hilbert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjlaaeffebib14\" id=\"fnref-apjlaaeffebib14\">2008</a>) shows that for a source at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a05.7, only 5% of the lensing optical depth is provided by galaxies with a halo mass lower than 7\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>11</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, comparable to J0439+1634's lensing galaxy (<em>v</em><sub><em>c</em></sub> = 160 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>) in the fiducial three-image lensing model. This implies up to ~20 lensed high-redshift quasars could have been missed in our survey due to contamination from lensing galaxy light. Benefiting from the boosted flux, an object such as J0439+1634 is a powerful probe of the physical properties of quasars and their host galaxies as well as serving as an ideal background source for studying high-redshift metal absorption lines and early IGM chemical enrichment.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We acknowledge the support of the staff at the MMT, <em>Magellan</em>, LBT, Keck, and Gemini Telescopes, and thank the Directors of LBTO, Gemini Observatory, JCMT, and STScI for granting us Director Discretionary time for follow-up observations of this object. X.F., J.Y., M.Y., and I.D.M. acknowledge support from US NSF grant AST-1515115, NASA ADAP grant NNX17AF28G, and <em>HST</em>-GO-13644 grant from the Space Telescope Science Institute. C.K. acknowledges support from US NSF grant AST-1716585. A.I.Z. acknowledges support from NSF grant AST-1211874. F.P. acknowledges support from the NASA <em>Chandra</em> award No. AR8-19021A and from the Yale Keck program No. Y144. B.P.V. and F.W. acknowledge funding through the ERC grants \"Cosmic Dawn\" and \"Cosmic Gas.\" R.W. and X.-B.W acknowledge support from NSFC grant No. 11533001.</p><p><em>Facilities:</em> UKIRT - United Kingdom Infrared Telescope, <em>WISE</em> - Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer, PS-1 - Panoramic Survey Telescope and Rapid Response System Telescope #1 (Pan-STARRS), MMT - MMT at Fred Lawrence Whipple Observatory, <em>Magellan</em> - , LBT - Large Binocular Telescope Observatory, Gemini - , Keck - , JCMT - James Clerk Maxwell Telescope, <em>HST</em> - Hubble Space Telescope satellite.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<!-- End Footnotes -->\t\n<!-- Start References List -->\n\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed It is not clear whether the discrepancy is due to limitations in the current data (e.g., in the <em>HST</em> PSF model) or to fundamental problems with this class of lens models. As an alternative, we consider the possibility that the lens galaxy could actually lie between the quasar images and be blended with them. In this scenario, the galaxy light detected in the <em>HST</em> image could be offset from the mass centroid, due perhaps to strong dust obscuration. For example, if the lensing galaxy is seen mostly edge-on, then we might have detected only the part of the galaxy with the highest surface brightness along the disk. The smallest residuals are obtained for a highly inclined galaxy with projected ellipticity <em>e</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.8, which produces four images and a total magnification of 10.4\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.2 (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjlaaeffef4\">4</a> and Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjlaaeffet1\">1</a>). <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/a56c0f0e-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c itemprop=\"description\">\n             <P>Strong gravitational lensing provides a powerful probe of the physical properties of quasars and their host galaxies. A high fraction of the most luminous high-redshift quasars was predicted to be lensed due to magnification bias. However, no multiple imaged quasar was found at <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05 in previous surveys. We report the discovery of J043947.08+163415.7, a strongly lensed quasar at <I>z</I>\u00a0=\u00a06.51, the first such object detected at the epoch of reionization, and the brightest quasar yet known at <I>z</I>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05. High-resolution <I>Hubble Space Telescope</I> imaging reveals a multiple imaged system with a maximum image separation <I>\u03b8</I>\u00a0~\u00a00<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2, best explained by a model of three quasar images lensed by a low-luminosity galaxy at <I>z</I>\u00a0~\u00a00.7, with a magnification factor of ~50. The existence of this source suggests that a significant population of strongly lensed, high-redshift quasars could have been missed by previous surveys, as standard color selection techniques would fail when the quasar color is contaminated by the lensing galaxy.</P> </div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=2041-8205/870/2/L11&doi=10.3847/2041-8213/aaeffe&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn
